            "content": "1. <b>Go</b>┬ж (<i>a</i>), тАШoxтАЩ or тАШcow.тАЩ<sup>1</sup> These were among the chief sources of wealth to the Vedic Indian, and are repeatedly referred to from the Rigveda onwards.<sup>2</sup> The milk (<b>Kс╣г─лra</b>) was either drunk fresh or made into butter (<b>Ghс╣Ыta</b>) or curds (<b>Dadhi</b>), or was mixed with <b>Soma</b> or used for cooking with grain (<b>Kс╣г─лraudana</b>). The cows were milked thrice a day, early (<i>pr─Бtar-doha</i>), in the forenoon (<b>Saс╣Гgava</b>), and in the evening (<i>s─Бyaс╣Г-doha</i>).<sup>3</sup> Thrice a day they were driven out to graze, according to the Taittir─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>4</sup> (<i>pr─Бtaс╕е, saс╣Гgave, s─Бyam</i>). The first milking was productive, the last two scanty.<sup>3</sup> According to the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>5</sup> among the Bharatas the herds in the evening are in the <b>Goс╣гс╣нha,</b> at midday in the <b>Saс╣Гgav─лn─л.</b> This passage S─Бyaс╣Зa expands by saying that the herds go home to the ┼Ъ─Бl─Б, or house for animals, at night so far as they consist of animals giving milk, while the others stayed out in the Goс╣гс╣нha, or open pasturage; but both were together in the cattle-shed during the heat of the day. The time before the Saс╣Гgava, when the cows were grazing freely on the pastureland, was called <b>Svasara.</b><sup>6</sup> When the cows were out feeding they were separated from the calves, which were, however, allowed to join them at the Saс╣Гgava,<sup>7</sup> and sometimes in the evening.<sup>8</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>While grazing the cattle were under the care of a herdsman (<b>Gop─Б, Gop─Бla</b>) armed with a goad,<sup>9</sup> but they were liable to all sorts of dangers, such as being lost, falling into pits, breaking limbs,<sup>10</sup> and being stolen. The marking of the ears of cattle was repeatedly adopted, no doubt, to indicate ownership.<sup>11</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>Large herds of cattle were well-known, as is shown by the D─Бnastutis, or тАШpraises of gifts,тАЩ in the Rigveda,<sup>12</sup> even when allowances are made for the exaggeration of priestly gratitude. The importance attached to the possession of cattle is shown<sup>13</sup> by the numerous passages in which the gods are asked to prosper them, and by the repeated prayers<sup>14</sup> for wealth in kine. Hence, too, forays for cattle (<b>Gaviс╣гс╣нi</b>) were well known; the Bharata host is called the тАШhorde desiring cowsтАЩ (<i>gavyan gr─Бmaс╕е</i>) in the Rigveda;<sup>15</sup> and a verbal root <i>gup,</i><sup>16</sup> тАШto protect,тАЩ was evolved as early as the Rigveda from the denominative <i>go-p─Бya,</i> тАШto guard cows.тАЩ The Vedic poets<sup>17</sup> do not hesitate to compare their songs with the lowing of cows, or to liken the choir of the singing Apsarases to cows.<sup>18</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>The cattle of the Vedic period were of many colours: red (<i>rohita</i>), light (<i>┼Ыukra</i>), dappled (<i>pс╣Ы┼Ыni</i>), even black (<i>kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa</i>).<sup>19</sup> Zimmer<sup>20</sup> sees a reference to cows with blazes on the face in one passage of the Rigveda,<sup>21</sup> but this is uncertain. <dvsn n='P'/>Oxen were regularly used for ploughing or for drawing wagons (<i>anaс╕Нv─Бh</i>), in which case they were, it seems, usually castrated.<sup>22</sup> Cows were not properly used for drawing carts, though they at times did so.<sup>23</sup> The flesh of both cows and bulls was sometimes eaten (<b>M─Бmsa</b>). Cattle were certainly the objects of individual ownership, and they formed one of the standards of exchange and valuation (see <b>Kraya</b>). <dvsn n='P'/>(<i>b</i>) The term <b>Go</b> is often applied to express the products of the cow. It frequently means the milk,<sup>1</sup> but rarely the flesh<sup>2</sup> of the animal. In many passages it designates leather used as the material of various objects, as a bowstring,<sup>3</sup> or a sling,<sup>4</sup> or thongs to fasten part of the chariot,<sup>5</sup> or reins,<sup>6</sup> or the lash of a whip.<sup>7</sup> See also <b>Carman,</b> with which Go is sometimes synonymous.<sup>8</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>(<i>c</i>) <b>G─Бvaс╕е</b> means the stars of heaven in two passages of the Rigveda,<sup>1</sup> according to Roth.<sup>2</sup> <F>1) i. 83, 1; 135, 8; ii. 23, 18, etc.; <i>g─Бva ukс╣гaс╣Зaс╕е,</i> i. 168, 2; Av. iii. 11, 8; <b>V─Бjasaс╣Зeyi</b> Saс╣Гhit─Б, xxi. 20; <i>g─Бvo</i> <i>dhenavaс╕е,</i> Rv. i. 173. 1; vi. 45. 28; x. 95, 6; V─Бjasaneyi Saс╣Гhit─Б, xxi. 19. etc.</F> <F>2) The five sacrificial animals are man, goat, sheep, ox, horse, S─Бс╣Еkh─Бyana ┼Ъrauta S┼лtra, ix. 23, 4; ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, ii. 4, 3, 13; iii. 1, 2, 13; iv. 5, 5, 10; xiv. 1, 1, 32.</F> <F>3) Taittir─лya Saс╣Гhit─Б, vii. 5, 3, 1.</F> <F>4) i. 4, 9, 2.
